			<h1 id="firstHeading" class="firstHeading" lang="en">Air pollution</h1>
									<div id="bodyContent" class="mw-body-content">
									<div id="siteSub">From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia</div>
								<div id="contentSub"></div>
												<div id="jump-to-nav" class="mw-jump">
					Jump to:					<a href="#mw-head">navigation</a>, 					<a href="#p-search">search</a>
				</div>
				<div id="mw-content-text" lang="en" dir="ltr" class="mw-content-ltr"><div role="note" class="hatnote">"Bad air quality" and "Air quality" redirect here. For the obsolete medical theory, see <a href="/wiki/Bad_air" class="mw-redirect" title="Bad air">Bad air</a>. For the measure of how polluted the air is, see <a href="/wiki/Air_quality_index" title="Air quality index">Air quality index</a>. For the properties of air, see <a href="/wiki/Qualities_of_air" class="mw-redirect" title="Qualities of air">Qualities of air</a>.</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:262px;"><a href="/wiki/File:AlfedPalmersmokestacks.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AlfedPalmersmokestacks.jpg/260px-AlfedPalmersmokestacks.jpg" width="260" height="201" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AlfedPalmersmokestacks.jpg/390px-AlfedPalmersmokestacks.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/aa/AlfedPalmersmokestacks.jpg/520px-AlfedPalmersmokestacks.jpg 2x" data-file-width="2000" data-file-height="1548" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:AlfedPalmersmokestacks.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Air pollution from a <a href="/wiki/Coke_(fuel)" title="Coke (fuel)">coking oven</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thermal-oxidizer-install.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Thermal-oxidizer-install.jpg/220px-Thermal-oxidizer-install.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Thermal-oxidizer-install.jpg/330px-Thermal-oxidizer-install.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b9/Thermal-oxidizer-install.jpg/440px-Thermal-oxidizer-install.jpg 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="720" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thermal-oxidizer-install.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
State-of-the-art process unit for <a href="/wiki/Thermal_oxidiser" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermal oxidiser">air pollution abatement</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p><b>Air pollution</b> occurs when harmful substances including <a href="/wiki/Particulates" title="Particulates">particulates</a> and <a href="/wiki/Biomolecule" title="Biomolecule">biological molecules</a> are introduced into <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_atmosphere" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth's atmosphere">Earth's atmosphere</a>. It may cause diseases, allergies or death in humans; it may also cause harm to other living organisms such as animals and food crops, and may damage the <a href="/wiki/Natural_environment" title="Natural environment">natural</a> or <a href="/wiki/Built_environment" title="Built environment">built environment</a>. Human activity and natural processes can both generate air pollution.</p>
<p>Indoor air pollution and poor urban air quality are listed as two of the world's worst toxic pollution problems in the 2008 <a href="/wiki/Blacksmith_Institute" class="mw-redirect" title="Blacksmith Institute">Blacksmith Institute</a> World's Worst Polluted Places report.<sup id="cite_ref-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup> According to the 2014 <a href="/wiki/WHO" class="mw-redirect" title="WHO">WHO</a> report, air pollution in 2012 caused the deaths of around 7 million people worldwide,<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> an estimate roughly matched by the <a href="/wiki/International_Energy_Agency" title="International Energy Agency">International Energy Agency</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup></p>

<p></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Pollutants">Pollutants</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Pollutants">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Pollutant" title="Pollutant">Pollutant</a> and <a href="/wiki/Greenhouse_gas" title="Greenhouse gas">Greenhouse gas</a></div>
<p>An air pollutant is a substance in the air that can have adverse effects on humans and the ecosystem. The substance can be solid particles, liquid droplets, or gases. A pollutant can be of natural origin or man-made. Pollutants are classified as primary or secondary. Primary pollutants are usually produced from a process, such as ash from a volcanic eruption. Other examples include <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> gas from motor vehicle exhaust, or the <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> released from factories. Secondary pollutants are not emitted directly. Rather, they form in the air when primary pollutants react or interact. <a href="/wiki/Ground_level_ozone" class="mw-redirect" title="Ground level ozone">Ground level ozone</a> is a prominent example of a secondary pollutant. Some pollutants may be both primary and secondary: they are both emitted directly and formed from other primary pollutants.</p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Air_pollution_by_industrial_chimneys.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Air_pollution_by_industrial_chimneys.jpg/220px-Air_pollution_by_industrial_chimneys.jpg" width="220" height="147" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Air_pollution_by_industrial_chimneys.jpg/330px-Air_pollution_by_industrial_chimneys.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Air_pollution_by_industrial_chimneys.jpg/440px-Air_pollution_by_industrial_chimneys.jpg 2x" data-file-width="750" data-file-height="500" /></a>

</div>
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Thermal-oxidizer-rto.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Thermal-oxidizer-rto.jpg/220px-Thermal-oxidizer-rto.jpg" width="220" height="179" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Thermal-oxidizer-rto.jpg/330px-Thermal-oxidizer-rto.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/ea/Thermal-oxidizer-rto.jpg/440px-Thermal-oxidizer-rto.jpg 2x" data-file-width="739" data-file-height="602" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Thermal-oxidizer-rto.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Thermal_oxidiser" class="mw-redirect" title="Thermal oxidiser">Thermal oxidizers</a> are air pollution abatement options for <a href="/wiki/Hazardous_air_pollutant" class="mw-redirect" title="Hazardous air pollutant">hazardous air pollutants</a> (HAPs), <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compounds" class="mw-redirect" title="Volatile organic compounds">volatile organic compounds</a> (VOCs), and odorous emissions.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Major primary pollutants produced by human activity include:</p>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">Carbon dioxide</a> (CO<sub>2</sub>) - This is by far the most emitted form of human caused air pollution. Although CO<sub>2</sub> is currently only about 405 parts per million in earth's atmosphere, billions of metric tons of CO<sub>2</sub> are emitted annually by burning of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup> CO<sub>2</sub> increase in earth's atmosphere has been accelerating.<sup id="cite_ref-MaunaMonthly_7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-MaunaMonthly-7">[7]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Sulfur_oxide" title="Sulfur oxide">Sulfur oxides</a> (SO<sub>x</sub>) - particularly sulfur dioxide, a chemical compound with the formula SO<sub>2</sub>. SO<sub>2</sub> is produced by volcanoes and in various industrial processes. Coal and petroleum often contain sulfur compounds, and their combustion generates sulfur dioxide. Further oxidation of SO<sub>2</sub>, usually in the presence of a catalyst such as NO<sub>2</sub>, forms H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>, and thus <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">acid rain</a>.[2] This is one of the causes for concern over the environmental impact of the use of these fuels as power sources.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">Nitrogen oxides</a> (NO<sub>x</sub>) - Nitrogen oxides, particularly <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_dioxide" title="Nitrogen dioxide">nitrogen dioxide</a>, are expelled from high temperature combustion, and are also produced during <a href="/wiki/Thunderstorms" class="mw-redirect" title="Thunderstorms">thunderstorms</a> by <a href="/wiki/Electric_discharge" title="Electric discharge">electric discharge</a>. They can be seen as a brown <a href="/wiki/Haze" title="Haze">haze</a> dome above or a <a href="/wiki/Plume_(hydrodynamics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Plume (hydrodynamics)">plume</a> downwind of cities. Nitrogen dioxide is a chemical compound with the formula NO<sub>2</sub>. It is one of several nitrogen oxides. One of the most prominent air pollutants, this reddish-brown toxic gas has a characteristic sharp, biting odor.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">Carbon monoxide</a> (CO) - CO is a colorless, odorless, toxic yet non-irritating gas. It is a product of <a href="/wiki/Incomplete_combustion" class="mw-redirect" title="Incomplete combustion">incomplete combustion</a> of fuel such as natural gas, coal or wood. Vehicular exhaust is a major source of carbon monoxide.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compounds" class="mw-redirect" title="Volatile organic compounds">Volatile organic compounds</a> (VOC) - VOCs are a well-known outdoor air pollutant. They are categorized as either methane (CH<sub>4</sub>) or non-methane (NMVOCs). Methane is an extremely efficient greenhouse gas which contributes to enhanced <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">global warming</a>. Other hydrocarbon VOCs are also significant greenhouse gases because of their role in creating ozone and prolonging the life of methane in the atmosphere. This effect varies depending on local air quality. The aromatic NMVOCs benzene, toluene and xylene are suspected carcinogens and may lead to leukemia with prolonged exposure. 1,3-butadiene is another dangerous compound often associated with industrial use.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Particulates" title="Particulates">Particulates</a>, alternatively referred to as particulate matter (PM), atmospheric particulate matter, or fine particles, are tiny particles of solid or liquid suspended in a gas. In contrast, aerosol refers to combined particles and gas. Some particulates occur naturally, originating from volcanoes, dust storms, forest and grassland fires, living vegetation, and sea spray. Human activities, such as the burning of fossil fuels in vehicles, power plants and various industrial processes also generate significant amounts of aerosols. Averaged worldwide, anthropogenic aerosols—those made by human activities—currently account for approximately 10 percent of our atmosphere. Increased levels of fine particles in the air are linked to health hazards such as heart disease,<sup id="cite_ref-8" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-9" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> altered lung function and lung cancer.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Radical_(chemistry)#Persistent_radicals" title="Radical (chemistry)">Persistent free radicals</a> connected to airborne fine particles are linked to cardiopulmonary disease.<sup id="cite_ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-11" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-11">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li>Toxic <a href="/wiki/Metal" title="Metal">metals</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">lead</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mercury_(element)" title="Mercury (element)">mercury</a>, especially their compounds.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Chlorofluorocarbons" class="mw-redirect" title="Chlorofluorocarbons">Chlorofluorocarbons</a> (CFCs) - harmful to the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a>; emitted from products are currently banned from use. These are gases which are released from air conditioners, refrigerators, aerosol sprays, etc. On release into the air, CFCs rise to the <a href="/wiki/Stratosphere" title="Stratosphere">stratosphere</a>. Here they come in contact with other gases and damage the <a href="/wiki/Ozone_layer" title="Ozone layer">ozone layer</a>. This allows harmful ultraviolet rays to reach the earth's surface. This can lead to skin cancer, eye disease and can even cause damage to plants.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ammonia" title="Ammonia">Ammonia</a> (NH<sub>3</sub>) - emitted from agricultural processes. Ammonia is a compound with the formula NH<sub>3</sub>. It is normally encountered as a gas with a characteristic pungent odor. Ammonia contributes significantly to the nutritional needs of terrestrial organisms by serving as a precursor to foodstuffs and fertilizers. Ammonia, either directly or indirectly, is also a building block for the synthesis of many pharmaceuticals. Although in wide use, ammonia is both caustic and hazardous. In the atmosphere, ammonia reacts with oxides of nitrogen and sulfur to form secondary particles.<sup id="cite_ref-12" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Odor" title="Odor">Odours</a>&#160;— such as from garbage, sewage, and industrial processes</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Radioactive_pollutants" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactive pollutants">Radioactive pollutants</a> - produced by <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_explosions" class="mw-redirect" title="Nuclear explosions">nuclear explosions</a>, nuclear events, war <a href="/wiki/Explosives" class="mw-redirect" title="Explosives">explosives</a>, and natural processes such as the <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> of <a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">radon</a>.</li>
</ul>
<p>Secondary pollutants include:</p>
<ul>
<li>Particulates created from gaseous primary pollutants and compounds in photochemical smog. <a href="/wiki/Smog" title="Smog">Smog</a> is a kind of air pollution. Classic smog results from large amounts of coal burning in an area caused by a mixture of smoke and sulfur dioxide. Modern smog does not usually come from coal but from vehicular and industrial emissions that are acted on in the atmosphere by <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">ultraviolet</a> light from the sun to form secondary pollutants that also combine with the primary emissions to form photochemical smog.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ground_level_ozone" class="mw-redirect" title="Ground level ozone">Ground level ozone</a> (O<sub>3</sub>) formed from NO<sub>x</sub> and VOCs. Ozone (O<sub>3</sub>) is a key constituent of the troposphere. It is also an important constituent of certain regions of the stratosphere commonly known as the Ozone layer. Photochemical and chemical reactions involving it drive many of the chemical processes that occur in the atmosphere by day and by night. At abnormally high concentrations brought about by human activities (largely the combustion of fossil fuel), it is a pollutant, and a constituent of smog.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Peroxyacetyl_nitrate" title="Peroxyacetyl nitrate">Peroxyacetyl nitrate</a> (C<sub>2</sub>H<sub>3</sub>NO<sub>5</sub>) - similarly formed from NO<sub>x</sub> and VOCs.</li>
</ul>
<p>Minor air pollutants include:</p>
<ul>
<li>A large number of minor <a href="/wiki/Category:Hazardous_air_pollutants" title="Category:Hazardous air pollutants">hazardous air pollutants</a>. Some of these are regulated in USA under the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Air_Act_(United_States)" title="Clean Air Act (United States)">Clean Air Act</a> and in Europe under the Air Framework Directive</li>
<li>A variety of <a href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutant" title="Persistent organic pollutant">persistent organic pollutants</a>, which can attach to particulates</li>
</ul>
<p>Persistent organic pollutants (POPs) are organic compounds that are resistant to environmental degradation through chemical, biological, and photolytic processes. Because of this, they have been observed to persist in the environment, to be capable of long-range transport, bioaccumulate in human and animal tissue, biomagnify in food chains, and to have potentially significant impacts on human health and the environment.</p>

<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Human_Fingerprint_on_Global_Air_Quality.webm" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
This video provides an overview of a NASA study on the human fingerprint on global air quality.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>There are various locations, activities or factors which are responsible for releasing pollutants into the atmosphere. These sources can be classified into two major categories.</p>
<p><b>Anthropogenic (man-made) sources:</b></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:BurningOffFieldsInTheEveningInSouthGeorgia.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/BurningOffFieldsInTheEveningInSouthGeorgia.jpg/220px-BurningOffFieldsInTheEveningInSouthGeorgia.jpg" width="220" height="116" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/BurningOffFieldsInTheEveningInSouthGeorgia.jpg/330px-BurningOffFieldsInTheEveningInSouthGeorgia.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/52/BurningOffFieldsInTheEveningInSouthGeorgia.jpg/440px-BurningOffFieldsInTheEveningInSouthGeorgia.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1024" data-file-height="539" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:BurningOffFieldsInTheEveningInSouthGeorgia.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Controlled_burning" class="mw-redirect" title="Controlled burning">Controlled burning</a> of a field outside of <a href="/wiki/Statesboro,_Georgia" title="Statesboro, Georgia">Statesboro</a>, <a href="/wiki/Georgia_(U.S._state)" title="Georgia (U.S. state)">Georgia</a> in preparation for spring planting.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>These are mostly related to the burning of multiple types of fuel.</p>
<ul>
<li><b>Stationary sources</b> include smoke stacks of <a href="/wiki/Power_plant" class="mw-redirect" title="Power plant">power plants</a>, manufacturing facilities (factories) and waste incinerators, as well as furnaces and other types of fuel-burning heating devices. In developing and poor countries, traditional biomass burning is the major source of air pollutants; traditional biomass includes wood, crop waste and dung.<sup id="cite_ref-13" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup></li>
<li><b>Mobile sources</b> include <a href="/wiki/Roadway_air_dispersion_modeling" title="Roadway air dispersion modeling">motor vehicles</a>, marine vessels, and aircraft.</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Controlled_burn" title="Controlled burn">Controlled burn</a></b> practices in agriculture and forest management. Controlled or prescribed burning is a technique sometimes used in forest management, farming, prairie restoration or greenhouse gas abatement. Fire is a natural part of both forest and grassland ecology and controlled fire can be a tool for foresters. Controlled burning stimulates the germination of some desirable forest trees, thus renewing the forest.</li>
<li><b>Fumes</b> from <a href="/wiki/Paint" title="Paint">paint</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hair_spray" title="Hair spray">hair spray</a>, <a href="/wiki/Varnish" title="Varnish">varnish</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aerosol_spray" title="Aerosol spray">aerosol sprays</a> and other solvents</li>
<li><b>Waste deposition</b> in <a href="/wiki/Landfill" title="Landfill">landfills</a>, which generate <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>. Methane is highly flammable and may form explosive mixtures with air. Methane is also an <a href="/wiki/Asphyxiant" class="mw-redirect" title="Asphyxiant">asphyxiant</a> and may displace oxygen in an enclosed space. Asphyxia or suffocation may result if the oxygen concentration is reduced to below 19.5% by displacement.</li>
<li><b>Military resources,</b> such as <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_weapon" title="Nuclear weapon">nuclear weapons</a>, <a href="/wiki/Toxic_gas" class="mw-redirect" title="Toxic gas">toxic gases</a>, <a href="/wiki/Germ_warfare" class="mw-redirect" title="Germ warfare">germ warfare</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rocket" title="Rocket">rocketry</a></li>
</ul>
<p><b>Natural sources:</b></p>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Dust_Storm_Texas_1935.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Dust_Storm_Texas_1935.jpg/220px-Dust_Storm_Texas_1935.jpg" width="220" height="144" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Dust_Storm_Texas_1935.jpg/330px-Dust_Storm_Texas_1935.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d9/Dust_Storm_Texas_1935.jpg/440px-Dust_Storm_Texas_1935.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3364" data-file-height="2196" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Dust_Storm_Texas_1935.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Dust storm approaching <a href="/wiki/Stratford,_Texas" title="Stratford, Texas">Stratford, Texas</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dust" title="Dust">Dust</a> from natural sources, usually large areas of land with little or no vegetation</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">Methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Flatulence" title="Flatulence">emitted</a> by the <a href="/wiki/Digestion" title="Digestion">digestion</a> of food by <a href="/wiki/Animal" title="Animal">animals</a>, for example <a href="/wiki/Cattle" title="Cattle">cattle</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Radon" title="Radon">Radon</a> gas from <a href="/wiki/Radioactive_decay" title="Radioactive decay">radioactive decay</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Earth%27s_crust" class="mw-redirect" title="Earth's crust">Earth's crust</a>. Radon is a colorless, odorless, naturally occurring, radioactive <a href="/wiki/Noble_gas" title="Noble gas">noble gas</a> that is formed from the decay of radium. It is considered to be a health hazard. Radon gas from natural sources can accumulate in buildings, especially in confined areas such as the basement and it is the second most frequent cause of lung cancer, after <a href="/wiki/Cigarette" title="Cigarette">cigarette</a> smoking.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Smoke" title="Smoke">Smoke</a> and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> from <a href="/wiki/Wildfires" class="mw-redirect" title="Wildfires">wildfires</a></li>
<li>Vegetation, in some regions, emits environmentally significant amounts of <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compounds" class="mw-redirect" title="Volatile organic compounds">Volatile organic compounds</a> (VOCs) on warmer days. These VOCs react with primary anthropogenic pollutants—specifically, NO<sub>x</sub>, SO<sub>2</sub>, and anthropogenic organic carbon compounds — to produce a seasonal haze of secondary pollutants.<sup id="cite_ref-15" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> Black gum, poplar, oak and willow are some examples of vegetation that can produce abundant VOCs. The VOC production from these species result in ozone levels up to eight times higher than the low-impact tree species.<sup id="cite_ref-16" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Volcano" title="Volcano">Volcanic</a> activity, which produces <a href="/wiki/Sulfur" title="Sulfur">sulfur</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chlorine" title="Chlorine">chlorine</a>, and ash particulates</li>
</ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Emission_factors">Emission factors</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Emission factors">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/AP_42_Compilation_of_Air_Pollutant_Emission_Factors" title="AP 42 Compilation of Air Pollutant Emission Factors">AP 42 Compilation of Air Pollutant Emission Factors</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:452px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Beijing_smog_comparison_August_2005.png" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Beijing_smog_comparison_August_2005.png/450px-Beijing_smog_comparison_August_2005.png" width="450" height="169" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Beijing_smog_comparison_August_2005.png/675px-Beijing_smog_comparison_August_2005.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a4/Beijing_smog_comparison_August_2005.png/900px-Beijing_smog_comparison_August_2005.png 2x" data-file-width="960" data-file-height="360" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Beijing_smog_comparison_August_2005.png" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a> air on a 2005-day after <a href="/wiki/Rain" title="Rain">rain</a> (left) and a smoggy day (right)</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>Air pollutant emission factors are reported representative values that attempt to relate the quantity of a pollutant released to the ambient air with an activity associated with the release of that pollutant. These factors are usually expressed as the weight of pollutant divided by a unit weight, volume, distance, or duration of the activity emitting the pollutant (e.g., kilograms of particulate emitted per <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">tonne</a> of coal burned). Such factors facilitate estimation of emissions from various sources of air pollution. In most cases, these factors are simply averages of all available data of acceptable quality, and are generally assumed to be representative of long-term averages.</p>
<p>There are 12 compounds in the list of <a href="/wiki/Persistent_organic_pollutant" title="Persistent organic pollutant">persistent organic pollutants</a>. Dioxins and furans are two of them and intentionally created by combustion of organics, like open burning of plastics. These compounds are also endocrine disruptors and can mutate the human genes.</p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">United States Environmental Protection Agency</a> has published a compilation of air pollutant emission factors for a wide range of industrial sources.<sup id="cite_ref-17" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a>, <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Canada" title="Canada">Canada</a> and many other countries have published similar compilations, as well as the <a href="/wiki/European_Environment_Agency" title="European Environment Agency">European Environment Agency</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-18" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-18">[18]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-19" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-21" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Exposure">Exposure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Exposure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Air pollution risk is a function of the hazard of the pollutant and the exposure to that pollutant. Air pollution exposure can be expressed for an individual, for certain groups (e.g. neighborhoods or children living in a country), or for entire populations. For example, one may want to calculate the exposure to a hazardous air pollutant for a geographic area, which includes the various microenvironments and age groups. This can be calculated<sup id="cite_ref-Daniel_A._Vallero_22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daniel_A._Vallero-22">[22]</a></sup> as an inhalation exposure. This would account for daily exposure in various settings (e.g. different indoor micro-environments and outdoor locations). The exposure needs to include different age and other demographic groups, especially infants, children, pregnant women and other sensitive subpopulations. The exposure to an air pollutant must integrate the concentrations of the air pollutant with respect to the time spent in each setting and the respective inhalation rates for each subgroup for each specific time that the subgroup is in the setting and engaged in particular activities (playing, cooking, reading, working, etc.). For example, a small child's inhalation rate will be less than that of an adult. A child engaged in vigorous exercise will have a higher respiration rate than the same child in a sedentary activity. The daily exposure, then, needs to reflect the time spent in each micro-environmental setting and the type of activities in these settings. The air pollutant concentration in each microactivity/microenvironmental setting is summed to indicate the exposure.<sup id="cite_ref-Daniel_A._Vallero_22-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daniel_A._Vallero-22">[22]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Indoor_air_quality_.28IAQ.29">Indoor air quality (IAQ)</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Indoor air quality (IAQ)">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Indoor_air_quality" title="Indoor air quality">Indoor air quality</a></div>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:New_Delhi_Met_Office,_India_02.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/New_Delhi_Met_Office%2C_India_02.jpg/220px-New_Delhi_Met_Office%2C_India_02.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/New_Delhi_Met_Office%2C_India_02.jpg/330px-New_Delhi_Met_Office%2C_India_02.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/New_Delhi_Met_Office%2C_India_02.jpg/440px-New_Delhi_Met_Office%2C_India_02.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3648" data-file-height="2736" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:New_Delhi_Met_Office,_India_02.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Air quality monitoring, <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi,_India" class="mw-redirect" title="New Delhi, India">New Delhi, India</a>.</div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A lack of ventilation indoors concentrates air pollution where people often spend the majority of their time. Radon (Rn) gas, a <a href="/wiki/Carcinogen" title="Carcinogen">carcinogen</a>, is exuded from the Earth in certain locations and trapped inside houses. Building materials including <a href="/wiki/Carpet" title="Carpet">carpeting</a> and <a href="/wiki/Plywood" title="Plywood">plywood</a> emit <a href="/wiki/Formaldehyde" title="Formaldehyde">formaldehyde</a> (H<sub>2</sub>CO) gas. Paint and solvents give off <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compounds" class="mw-redirect" title="Volatile organic compounds">volatile organic compounds</a> (VOCs) as they dry. <a href="/wiki/Lead" title="Lead">Lead</a> paint can degenerate into <a href="/wiki/Dust" title="Dust">dust</a> and be inhaled. Intentional air pollution is introduced with the use of <a href="/wiki/Air_freshener" title="Air freshener">air fresheners</a>, <a href="/wiki/Incense" title="Incense">incense</a>, and other scented items. Controlled wood fires in stoves and <a href="/wiki/Fireplace" title="Fireplace">fireplaces</a> can add significant amounts of smoke particulates into the air, inside and out.<sup id="cite_ref-23" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup> Indoor pollution fatalities may be caused by using <a href="/wiki/Pesticide" title="Pesticide">pesticides</a> and other chemical sprays indoors without proper ventilation.</p>
<p>Carbon monoxide poisoning and fatalities are often caused by faulty vents and chimneys, or by the burning of <a href="/wiki/Charcoal" title="Charcoal">charcoal</a> indoors or in a confined space, such as a tent.<sup id="cite_ref-24" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> Chronic <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide_poisoning" title="Carbon monoxide poisoning">carbon monoxide poisoning</a> can result even from poorly-adjusted <a href="/wiki/Pilot_light" title="Pilot light">pilot lights</a>. Traps are built into all domestic <a href="/wiki/Plumbing" title="Plumbing">plumbing</a> to keep sewer gas and <a href="/wiki/Hydrogen_sulfide" title="Hydrogen sulfide">hydrogen sulfide</a>, out of interiors. Clothing emits <a href="/wiki/Tetrachloroethylene" title="Tetrachloroethylene">tetrachloroethylene</a>, or other dry cleaning fluids, for days after <a href="/wiki/Dry_cleaning" title="Dry cleaning">dry cleaning</a>.</p>
<p>Though its use has now been banned in many countries, the extensive use of <a href="/wiki/Asbestos" title="Asbestos">asbestos</a> in industrial and domestic environments in the past has left a potentially very dangerous material in many localities. <a href="/wiki/Asbestosis" title="Asbestosis">Asbestosis</a> is a chronic <a href="/wiki/Inflammation" title="Inflammation">inflammatory</a> medical condition affecting the tissue of the <a href="/wiki/Lung" title="Lung">lungs</a>. It occurs after long-term, heavy exposure to asbestos from asbestos-containing materials in structures. Sufferers have severe <a href="/wiki/Dyspnea" class="mw-redirect" title="Dyspnea">dyspnea</a> (shortness of breath) and are at an increased risk regarding several different types of <a href="/wiki/Lung_cancer" title="Lung cancer">lung cancer</a>. As clear explanations are not always stressed in non-technical literature, care should be taken to distinguish between several forms of relevant diseases. According to the <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.euro.who.int/__data/assets/pdf_file/0015/123072/AQG2ndEd_6_2_asbestos.PDF">World Health Organisation (WHO)</a>, these may defined as; <a href="/wiki/Asbestosis" title="Asbestosis">asbestosis</a>, <i>lung cancer</i>, and <i><a href="/wiki/Peritoneal_Mesothelioma" class="mw-redirect" title="Peritoneal Mesothelioma">Peritoneal Mesothelioma</a></i> (generally a very rare form of cancer, when more widespread it is almost always associated with prolonged exposure to asbestos).</p>
<p>Biological sources of air pollution are also found indoors, as gases and airborne particulates. <a href="/wiki/Pet" title="Pet">Pets</a> produce dander, people produce dust from minute skin flakes and decomposed hair, <a href="/wiki/House_dust_mite" title="House dust mite">dust mites</a> in bedding, carpeting and furniture produce enzymes and micrometre-sized fecal droppings, inhabitants emit methane, <a href="/wiki/Mold" title="Mold">mold</a> forms on walls and generates <a href="/wiki/Mycotoxins" class="mw-redirect" title="Mycotoxins">mycotoxins</a> and spores, <a href="/wiki/Air_conditioning" title="Air conditioning">air conditioning</a> systems can incubate <a href="/wiki/Legionellosis" class="mw-redirect" title="Legionellosis">Legionnaires' disease</a> and mold, and <a href="/wiki/Houseplant" title="Houseplant">houseplants</a>, soil and surrounding <a href="/wiki/Gardens" class="mw-redirect" title="Gardens">gardens</a> can produce <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a>, dust, and mold. Indoors, the lack of air circulation allows these airborne pollutants to accumulate more than they would otherwise occur in nature.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Health_effects">Health effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Health effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/Neuroplastic_effects_of_pollution" title="Neuroplastic effects of pollution">Neuroplastic effects of pollution</a></div>
<p>Air pollution is a significant risk factor for a number of <a href="/wiki/List_of_pollution-related_diseases" title="List of pollution-related diseases">pollution-related diseases</a> and health conditions including respiratory infections, heart disease, <a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">COPD</a>, stroke and lung cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> The health effects caused by air pollution may include difficulty in breathing, wheezing, coughing, <a href="/wiki/Asthma" title="Asthma">asthma</a> and worsening of existing respiratory and cardiac conditions. These effects can result in increased medication use, increased doctor or emergency room visits, more hospital admissions and premature death. The human health effects of poor air quality are far reaching, but principally affect the body's respiratory system and the cardiovascular system. Individual reactions to air pollutants depend on the type of pollutant a person is exposed to, the degree of exposure, and the individual's health status and genetics.<sup id="cite_ref-Daniel_A._Vallero_22-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Daniel_A._Vallero-22">[22]</a></sup> The most common sources of air pollution include particulates, ozone, nitrogen dioxide, and sulphur dioxide. Children aged less than five years that live in developing countries are the most vulnerable population in terms of total deaths attributable to indoor and outdoor air pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-25" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Mortality">Mortality</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Mortality">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/World_Health_Organization" title="World Health Organization">World Health Organization</a> estimated in 2014 that every year air pollution causes the premature death of some 7 million people worldwide.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup> India has the highest death rate due to air pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-26" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup> India also has more deaths from asthma than any other nation according to the World Health Organization. In December 2013 air pollution was estimated to kill 500,000 people in China each year.<sup id="cite_ref-27" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup> There is a positive correlation between <a href="/wiki/Pneumonia" title="Pneumonia">pneumonia</a>-related deaths and air pollution from motor vehicle emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-28" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>
<p>Annual premature European deaths caused by air pollution are estimated at 430,000.<sup id="cite_ref-europarl.europa.eu_29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europarl.europa.eu-29">[29]</a></sup> An important cause of these deaths is nitrogen dioxide and other nitrogen oxides (NOx) emitted by road vehicles.<sup id="cite_ref-europarl.europa.eu_29-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-europarl.europa.eu-29">[29]</a></sup> Across the <a href="/wiki/European_Union" title="European Union">European Union</a>, air pollution is estimated to reduce <a href="/wiki/Life_expectancy" title="Life expectancy">life expectancy</a> by almost nine months.<sup id="cite_ref-30" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> Causes of deaths include <a href="/wiki/Stroke" title="Stroke">strokes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heart_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Heart disease">heart disease</a>, COPD, lung cancer, and lung infections.<sup id="cite_ref-WHO2014_3-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-WHO2014-3">[3]</a></sup></p>
<p>Urban outdoor air pollution is estimated to cause 1.3 million deaths worldwide per year. Children are particularly at risk due to the immaturity of their respiratory organ systems.<sup id="cite_ref-31" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup></p>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/United_States_Environmental_Protection_Agency" title="United States Environmental Protection Agency">US EPA</a> estimates that a proposed set of changes in <a href="/wiki/Diesel_engine" title="Diesel engine">diesel engine</a> technology (<i>Tier 2</i>) could result in 12,000 fewer <i>premature mortalities</i>, 15,000 fewer <a href="/wiki/Myocardial_infarction" title="Myocardial infarction">heart attacks</a>, 6,000 fewer <a href="/wiki/Emergency_room" class="mw-redirect" title="Emergency room">emergency room</a> visits by children with asthma, and 8,900 fewer respiratory-related hospital admissions each year in the United States.<sup id="cite_ref-32" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>
<p>The US EPA has estimated that limiting ground-level ozone concentration to 65 parts per billion, would avert 1,700 to 5,100 premature deaths nationwide in 2020 compared with the 75-ppb standard. The agency projected the more protective standard would also prevent an additional 26,000 cases of aggravated asthma, and more than a million cases of missed work or school.<sup id="cite_ref-33" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-34" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> Following this assessment, the EPA acted to protect public health by lowering the National Ambient Air Quality Standards (NAAQS) for ground-level ozone to 70 parts per billion (ppb).<sup id="cite_ref-35" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup></p>
<p>A new economic study of the health impacts and associated costs of air pollution in the <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles_Basin" title="Los Angeles Basin">Los Angeles Basin</a> and <a href="/wiki/San_Joaquin_Valley" title="San Joaquin Valley">San Joaquin Valley</a> of Southern California shows that more than 3,800 people die prematurely (approximately 14 years earlier than normal) each year because air pollution levels violate federal standards. The number of annual premature deaths is considerably higher than the fatalities related to auto collisions in the same area, which average fewer than 2,000 per year.<sup id="cite_ref-36" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-37" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-37">[37]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-38" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-38">[38]</a></sup></p>
<p>Diesel exhaust (DE) is a major contributor to combustion-derived particulate matter air pollution. In several human experimental studies, using a well-validated exposure chamber setup, DE has been linked to acute vascular dysfunction and increased thrombus formation.<sup id="cite_ref-39" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-39">[39]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-40" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup></p>
<p>The mechanisms linking air pollution to increased cardiovascular mortality are uncertain, but probably include pulmonary and systemic inflammation.<sup id="cite_ref-41" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-41">[41]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cardiovascular_disease">Cardiovascular disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Cardiovascular disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>A 2007 review of evidence found ambient air pollution exposure is a risk factor correlating with increased total mortality from cardiovascular events (range: 12% to 14% per 10 microg/m<sup>3</sup> increase).<sup id="cite_ref-42" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup></p>
<p>Air pollution is also emerging as a risk factor for stroke, particularly in developing countries where pollutant levels are highest.<sup id="cite_ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> A 2007 study found that in women, air pollution is not associated with hemorrhagic but with ischemic stroke.<sup id="cite_ref-44" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup> Air pollution was also found to be associated with increased incidence and mortality from coronary stroke in a cohort study in 2011.<sup id="cite_ref-45" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Associations are believed to be causal and effects may be mediated by vasoconstriction, low-grade inflammation and <a href="/wiki/Atherosclerosis" title="Atherosclerosis">atherosclerosis</a><sup id="cite_ref-Provost_46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Provost-46">[46]</a></sup> Other mechanisms such as autonomic nervous system imbalance have also been suggested.<sup id="cite_ref-AmericanHeartAssociation_47-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-AmericanHeartAssociation-47">[47]</a></sup> <sup id="cite_ref-Louwies_48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Louwies-48">[48]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Lung_disease">Lung disease</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Lung disease">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/Chronic_obstructive_pulmonary_disease" title="Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease">Chronic obstructive pulmonary disease</a> (COPD) includes diseases such as chronic bronchitis and emphysema.<sup id="cite_ref-49" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup></p>
<p>Research has demonstrated increased risk of developing asthma<sup id="cite_ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup> and COPD<sup id="cite_ref-51" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> from increased exposure to traffic-related air pollution. Additionally, air pollution has been associated with increased hospitalization and mortality from asthma and COPD.<sup id="cite_ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-53" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup></p>
<p>A study conducted in 1960-1961 in the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Great_Smog" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Smog">Great Smog</a> of 1952 compared 293 London residents with 477 residents of Gloucester, Peterborough, and Norwich, three towns with low reported death rates from chronic bronchitis. All subjects were male postal truck drivers aged 40 to 59. Compared to the subjects from the outlying towns, the London subjects exhibited more severe respiratory symptoms (including cough, phlegm, and dyspnea), reduced lung function (<a href="/wiki/FEV1" class="mw-redirect" title="FEV1">FEV<sub>1</sub></a> and peak flow rate), and increased sputum production and purulence. The differences were more pronounced for subjects aged 50 to 59. The study controlled for age and smoking habits, so concluded that air pollution was the most likely cause of the observed differences.<sup id="cite_ref-54" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-54">[54]</a></sup></p>
<p>It is believed that much like <a href="/wiki/Cystic_fibrosis" title="Cystic fibrosis">cystic fibrosis</a>, by living in a more urban environment serious health hazards become more apparent. Studies have shown that in urban areas patients suffer <a href="/wiki/Mucus" title="Mucus">mucus</a> hypersecretion, lower levels of lung function, and more self-diagnosis of chronic bronchitis and emphysema.<sup id="cite_ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Cancer">Cancer</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Cancer">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:152px;"><a href="/wiki/File:DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg/150px-DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg" width="150" height="229" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg/225px-DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4f/DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg/300px-DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1911" data-file-height="2919" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:DARK_CLOUDS_OF_FACTORY_SMOKE_OBSCURE_CLARK_AVENUE_BRIDGE_-_NARA_-_550179.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
<a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">Cancer</a> mainly the result of <a href="/wiki/Environmental_factor" title="Environmental factor">environmental factors</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-20151217_56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20151217-56">[56]</a></sup></div>
</div>
</div>
<p>A review of evidence regarding whether ambient air pollution exposure is a risk factor for cancer in 2007 found solid data to conclude that long-term exposure to PM2.5 (fine particulates) increases the overall risk of non-accidental mortality by 6% per a 10 microg/m<sup>3</sup> increase. Exposure to PM2.5 was also associated with an increased risk of mortality from lung cancer (range: 15% to 21% per 10 microg/m<sup>3</sup> increase) and total cardiovascular mortality (range: 12% to 14% per a 10 microg/m<sup>3</sup> increase). The review further noted that living close to busy traffic appears to be associated with elevated risks of these three outcomes --- increase in lung cancer deaths, cardiovascular deaths, and overall non-accidental deaths. The reviewers also found suggestive evidence that exposure to PM2.5 is positively associated with mortality from coronary heart diseases and exposure to SO<sub>2</sub> increases mortality from lung cancer, but the data was insufficient to provide solid conclusions.<sup id="cite_ref-57" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> Another investigation showed that higher activity level increases deposition fraction of aerosol particles in human lung and recommended avoiding heavy activities like running in outdoor space at polluted areas.<sup id="cite_ref-Saber2012_58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Saber2012-58">[58]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2011, a large Danish epidemiological study found an increased risk of lung cancer for patients who lived in areas with high nitrogen oxide concentrations. In this study, the association was higher for non-smokers than smokers.<sup id="cite_ref-59" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-59">[59]</a></sup> An additional Danish study, also in 2011, likewise noted evidence of possible associations between air pollution and other forms of cancer, including cervical cancer and brain cancer.<sup id="cite_ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>
<p>In December 2015, medical scientists reported that <a href="/wiki/Cancer" title="Cancer">cancer</a> is overwhelmingly a result of <a href="/wiki/Environmental_factor" title="Environmental factor">environmental factors</a>, and not largely down to bad luck.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-20151217_56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20151217-56">[56]</a></sup> Maintaining a healthy weight, eating a healthy diet, minimizing alcohol and eliminating smoking reduces the risk of developing the disease, according to the researchers.<sup id="cite_ref-BBC-20151217_56-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BBC-20151217-56">[56]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Children">Children</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Children">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In the United States, despite the passage of the <a href="/wiki/Clean_Air_Act_(United_States)" title="Clean Air Act (United States)">Clean Air Act</a> in 1970, in 2002 at least 146 million Americans were living in <a href="/wiki/Non-Attainment_Area" class="mw-redirect" title="Non-Attainment Area">non-attainment areas</a>—regions in which the concentration of certain air pollutants exceeded federal standards.<sup id="cite_ref-61" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> These dangerous pollutants are known as the <a href="/wiki/Criteria_pollutant" class="mw-redirect" title="Criteria pollutant">criteria pollutants</a>, and include ozone, <a href="/wiki/Particulate_matter" class="mw-redirect" title="Particulate matter">particulate matter</a>, sulfur dioxide, nitrogen dioxide, carbon monoxide, and lead. Protective measures to ensure children's health are being taken in cities such as <a href="/wiki/New_Delhi" title="New Delhi">New Delhi</a>, India where buses now use <a href="/wiki/Compressed_natural_gas" title="Compressed natural gas">compressed natural gas</a> to help eliminate the "pea-soup" smog.<sup id="cite_ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> A recent study in Europe has found that exposure to <a href="/wiki/Ultrafine_particle" title="Ultrafine particle">ultrafine</a> particles can increase <a href="/wiki/Blood_pressure" title="Blood pressure">blood pressure</a> in children.<sup id="cite_ref-UFPBPChildren_63-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-UFPBPChildren-63">[63]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id=".22Clean.22_areas">"Clean" areas</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: &quot;Clean&quot; areas">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Even in the areas with relatively low levels of air pollution, public health effects can be significant and costly, since a large number of people breathe in such pollutants. A 2005 scientific study for the British Columbia Lung Association showed that a small improvement in air quality (1% reduction of ambient PM2.5 and ozone concentrations) would produce $29 million in annual savings in the <a href="/wiki/Metro_Vancouver" class="mw-redirect" title="Metro Vancouver">Metro Vancouver</a> region in 2010.<sup id="cite_ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup> This finding is based on health valuation of lethal (death) and sub-lethal (illness) affects.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Central_nervous_system">Central nervous system</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Central nervous system">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Data is accumulating that air pollution exposure also affects the <a href="/wiki/Central_nervous_system" title="Central nervous system">central nervous system</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-bos_65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-bos-65">[65]</a></sup></p>
<p>In a June 2014 study conducted by researchers at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Rochester" title="University of Rochester">University of Rochester</a> Medical Center, published in the journal <a href="/wiki/Environmental_Health_Perspectives" title="Environmental Health Perspectives">Environmental Health Perspectives</a>, it was discovered that early exposure to air pollution causes the same damaging changes in the brain as <a href="/wiki/Autism" title="Autism">autism</a> and <a href="/wiki/Schizophrenia" title="Schizophrenia">schizophrenia</a>. The study also shows that air pollution also affected short-term memory, learning ability, and impulsivity. Lead researcher Professor Deborah Cory-Slechta said that "When we looked closely at the ventricles, we could see that the white matter that normally surrounds them hadn't fully developed. It appears that inflammation had damaged those brain cells and prevented that region of the brain from developing, and the ventricles simply expanded to fill the space. Our findings add to the growing body of evidence that air pollution may play a role in autism, as well as in other neurodevelopmental disorders." Air pollution has a more significant negative effect on males than on females.<sup id="cite_ref-Innes-effects_66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Innes-effects-66">[66]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-McEnaney-effects_67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McEnaney-effects-67">[67]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-University_of_Rochester_68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-University_of_Rochester-68">[68]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2015, experimental studies reported the detection of significant episodic (situational) cognitive impairment from impurities in indoor air breathed by test subjects who were not informed about changes in the air quality. Researchers at the Harvard University and SUNY Upstate Medical University and Syracuse University measured the cognitive performance of 24 participants in three different controlled laboratory atmospheres that simulated those found in "conventional" and "green" buildings, as well as green buildings with enhanced ventilation. Performance was evaluated objectively using the widely used Strategic Management Simulation software simulation tool, which is a well-validated assessment test for executive decision-making in an unconstrained situation allowing initiative and improvisation. Significant deficits were observed in the performance scores achieved in increasing concentrations of either <a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compound" title="Volatile organic compound">volatile organic compounds</a> (VOCs) or <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide" title="Carbon dioxide">carbon dioxide</a>, while keeping other factors constant. The highest impurity levels reached are not uncommon in some classroom or office environments.<sup id="cite_ref-NYT_69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NYT-69">[69]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-EHP_70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EHP-70">[70]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Agricultural_effects">Agricultural effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Agricultural effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In India in 2014, it was reported that air pollution by <a href="/wiki/Black_carbon" title="Black carbon">black carbon</a> and ground level ozone had cut crop yields in the most affected areas by almost half in 2010 when compared to 1980 levels.<sup id="cite_ref-71" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Economic_effects">Economic effects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Economic effects">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Air pollution costs the world economy $5 trillion per year as a result of productivity losses and degraded quality of life, according to a joint study by the <a href="/wiki/World_Bank" title="World Bank">World Bank</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Institute_for_Health_Metrics_and_Evaluation" title="Institute for Health Metrics and Evaluation">Institute for Health Metrics and Evaluation</a> (IHME) at the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Washington" title="University of Washington">University of Washington</a><sup id="cite_ref-commondreams.org_72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-commondreams.org-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-documents.worldbank.org_73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-documents.worldbank.org-73">[73]</a></sup> These productivity losses are caused by deaths due to diseases caused by air pollution. One out of ten deaths in 2013 was caused by diseases associated with air pollution and the problem is getting worse. The problem is even more acute in the <a href="/wiki/Developing_world" class="mw-redirect" title="Developing world">developing world</a>. "Children under age 5 in lower-income countries are more than 60 times as likely to die from exposure to air pollution as children in high-income countries."<sup id="cite_ref-commondreams.org_72-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-commondreams.org-72">[72]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-documents.worldbank.org_73-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-documents.worldbank.org-73">[73]</a></sup> The report states that additional economic losses caused by air pollution, including health costs and the adverse effect on agricultural and other productivity were not calculated in the report, and thus the actual costs to the world economy are far higher than $5 trillion.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Historical_disasters">Historical disasters</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Historical disasters">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The world's worst short-term civilian pollution crisis was the 1984 <a href="/wiki/Bhopal_Disaster" class="mw-redirect" title="Bhopal Disaster">Bhopal Disaster</a> in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-74" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> Leaked industrial vapours from the Union Carbide factory, belonging to Union Carbide, Inc., U.S.A. (later bought by <a href="/wiki/Dow_Chemical_Company" title="Dow Chemical Company">Dow Chemical Company</a>), killed at least 3787 people and injured from 150,000 to 600,000. The United Kingdom suffered its worst air pollution event when the December 4 <a href="/wiki/Great_Smog" class="mw-redirect" title="Great Smog">Great Smog</a> of 1952 formed over <a href="/wiki/London" title="London">London</a>. In six days more than 4,000 died and more recent estimates put the figure at nearer 12,000.<sup id="cite_ref-EHP_112_1_75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EHP_112_1-75">[75]</a></sup> An <a href="/wiki/Sverdlovsk_anthrax_leak" title="Sverdlovsk anthrax leak">accidental leak</a> of <a href="/wiki/Anthrax" title="Anthrax">anthrax</a> spores from a <a href="/wiki/Biological_warfare" title="Biological warfare">biological warfare</a> laboratory in the former <a href="/wiki/USSR" class="mw-redirect" title="USSR">USSR</a> in 1979 near <a href="/wiki/Yekaterinburg" title="Yekaterinburg">Sverdlovsk</a> is believed to have caused at least 64 deaths.<sup id="cite_ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> The worst single incident of air pollution to occur in the US occurred in <a href="/wiki/Donora,_Pennsylvania" title="Donora, Pennsylvania">Donora, Pennsylvania</a> in late October, 1948, when 20 people died and over 7,000 were injured.<sup id="cite_ref-77" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Alternatives_to_pollution">Alternatives to pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=17" title="Edit section: Alternatives to pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are now practical alternatives to the three principal causes of air pollution.</p>
<ul>
<li>Combustion of fossil fuels for space heating can be replaced by using <a href="/wiki/Ground_source_heat_pumps" class="mw-redirect" title="Ground source heat pumps">ground source heat pumps</a> and <a href="/wiki/Seasonal_thermal_energy_storage" title="Seasonal thermal energy storage">seasonal thermal energy storage</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-78" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></li>
<li>Electric power generation from burning fossil fuels can be replaced by power generation from nuclear and renewables.</li>
<li>Motor vehicles driven by fossil fuels, a key factor in urban air pollution, can be replaced by electric vehicles.</li>
</ul>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Reduction_efforts">Reduction efforts</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=18" title="Edit section: Reduction efforts">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>There are various air pollution control technologies and strategies available to reduce air pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup><sup id="cite_ref-80" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> At its most basic level, <a href="/wiki/Land-use_planning" title="Land-use planning">land-use planning</a> is likely to involve zoning and transport infrastructure planning. In most developed countries, land-use planning is an important part of social policy, ensuring that land is used efficiently for the benefit of the wider economy and population, as well as to protect the environment.</p>
<p>Because a large share of air pollution is caused by combustion of <a href="/wiki/Fossil_fuels" class="mw-redirect" title="Fossil fuels">fossil fuels</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Coal" title="Coal">coal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Oil" title="Oil">oil</a>, the reduction of these fuels can reduce air pollution drastically. Most effective is the switch to clean power sources such as <a href="/wiki/Wind_power" title="Wind power">wind power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Solar_power" title="Solar power">solar power</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hydro_power" class="mw-redirect" title="Hydro power">hydro power</a> which don't cause air pollution.<sup id="cite_ref-Jacobson_81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobson-81">[81]</a></sup> Efforts to reduce pollution from mobile sources includes primary regulation (many developing countries have permissive regulations),<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2008)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> expanding regulation to new sources (such as cruise and transport ships, farm equipment, and small gas-powered equipment such as <a href="/wiki/String_trimmer" title="String trimmer">string trimmers</a>, <a href="/wiki/Chainsaw" title="Chainsaw">chainsaws</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Snowmobiles" class="mw-redirect" title="Snowmobiles">snowmobiles</a>), increased fuel efficiency (such as through the use of <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_vehicle" title="Hybrid vehicle">hybrid vehicles</a>), conversion to cleaner fuels or conversion to <a href="/wiki/Electric_vehicle" title="Electric vehicle">electric vehicles</a>.</p>
<p><a href="/wiki/Titanium_dioxide" title="Titanium dioxide">Titanium dioxide</a> has been researched for its ability to reduce air pollution. <a href="/wiki/Ultraviolet" title="Ultraviolet">Ultraviolet</a> light will release free electrons from material, thereby creating free radicals, which break up VOCs and NOx gases. One form is <a href="/wiki/Superhydrophilicity" title="Superhydrophilicity">superhydrophilic</a>.<sup id="cite_ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup></p>
<p>In 2014, <a href="/wiki/Tony_Ryan_(scientist)" title="Tony Ryan (scientist)">Prof. Tony Ryan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Simon_Armitage" title="Simon Armitage">Prof. Simon Armitage</a> of <a href="/wiki/University_of_Sheffield" title="University of Sheffield">University of Sheffield</a> prepared a 10 meter by 20 meter-sized poster coated with microscopic, pollution-eating nanoparticles of titanium dioxide. Placed on a building, this giant poster can absorb the toxic emission from around 20 cars each day.<sup id="cite_ref-83" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup></p>
<p>A very effective means to reduce air pollution is the <a href="/wiki/Energy_transition" title="Energy transition">transition</a> to <a href="/wiki/Renewable_energy" title="Renewable energy">renewable energy</a>. According to a study published in <a href="/wiki/Energy_and_Environmental_Science" title="Energy and Environmental Science">Energy and Environmental Science</a> in 2015 the switch to <a href="/wiki/100%25_renewable_energy" title="100% renewable energy">100% renewable energy</a> in the United States would eliminate about 62,000 premature mortalities per year and about 42,000 in 2050, if no biomass were used. This would save about $600 billion in <a href="/wiki/Health_costs" class="mw-redirect" title="Health costs">health costs</a> a year due to reduced air pollution in 2050, or about 3.6% of the 2014 U.S. gross domestic product.<sup id="cite_ref-Jacobson_81-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jacobson-81">[81]</a></sup></p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Control_devices">Control devices</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=19" title="Edit section: Control devices">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>The following items are commonly used as pollution control devices in industry and transportation. They can either destroy <a href="/wiki/Contaminant" class="mw-redirect" title="Contaminant">contaminants</a> or remove them from an exhaust stream before it is emitted into the atmosphere.</p>
<div class="div-col columns column-count column-count-2" style="-moz-column-count: 2; -webkit-column-count: 2; column-count: 2;">
<ul>
<li><b>Particulate control</b>
<ul>
<li>Mechanical collectors (<a href="/wiki/Cyclonic_separation" title="Cyclonic separation">dust cyclones</a>, multicyclones)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Electrostatic_precipitator" title="Electrostatic precipitator">Electrostatic precipitators</a> An electrostatic precipitator (ESP), or electrostatic air cleaner is a particulate collection device that removes particles from a flowing gas (such as air), using the force of an induced electrostatic charge. Electrostatic precipitators are highly efficient filtration devices that minimally impede the flow of gases through the device, and can easily remove fine particulates such as dust and smoke from the air stream.</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Dust_collector#Types_of_baghouses" title="Dust collector">Baghouses</a> Designed to handle heavy dust loads, a dust collector consists of a blower, dust filter, a filter-cleaning system, and a dust receptacle or dust removal system (distinguished from air cleaners which utilize disposable filters to remove the dust).</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wet_scrubber" title="Wet scrubber">Particulate scrubbers</a> Wet scrubber is a form of pollution control technology. The term describes a variety of devices that use pollutants from a furnace flue gas or from other gas streams. In a wet scrubber, the polluted gas stream is brought into contact with the scrubbing liquid, by spraying it with the liquid, by forcing it through a pool of liquid, or by some other contact method, so as to remove the pollutants.</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Scrubber" title="Scrubber">Scrubbers</a></b>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Baffle_spray_scrubber" title="Baffle spray scrubber">Baffle spray scrubber</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cyclonic_spray_scrubber" title="Cyclonic spray scrubber">Cyclonic spray scrubber</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Ejector_venturi_scrubber" title="Ejector venturi scrubber">Ejector venturi scrubber</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Mechanically_aided_scrubber" title="Mechanically aided scrubber">Mechanically aided scrubber</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Spray_tower" title="Spray tower">Spray tower</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wet_scrubber" title="Wet scrubber">Wet scrubber</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_oxide" title="Nitrogen oxide">NOx control</a></b>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/LO-NOx_burner" title="LO-NOx burner">Low NOx burners</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Selective_catalytic_reduction" title="Selective catalytic reduction">Selective catalytic reduction</a> (SCR)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Selective_non-catalytic_reduction" title="Selective non-catalytic reduction">Selective non-catalytic reduction</a> (SNCR)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wet_scrubber" title="Wet scrubber">NOx scrubbers</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Exhaust_gas_recirculation" title="Exhaust gas recirculation">Exhaust gas recirculation</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Catalytic_converter" title="Catalytic converter">Catalytic converter</a> (also for VOC control)</li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Volatile_organic_compound" title="Volatile organic compound">VOC abatement</a></b>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Adsorption" title="Adsorption">Adsorption systems</a>, using <a href="/wiki/Activated_carbon" title="Activated carbon">activated carbon</a>, such as <a href="/wiki/Fluidized_bed_concentrator" title="Fluidized bed concentrator">Fluidized Bed Concentrator</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Gas_flare" title="Gas flare">Flares</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Thermal_oxidizer" title="Thermal oxidizer">Thermal oxidizers</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Catalytic_converter" title="Catalytic converter">Catalytic converters</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Biofilter" title="Biofilter">Biofilters</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wet_scrubber" title="Wet scrubber">Absorption (scrubbing)</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Cryogenic" class="mw-redirect" title="Cryogenic">Cryogenic</a> <a href="/wiki/Condenser_(heat_transfer)" title="Condenser (heat transfer)">condensers</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vapor_recovery" title="Vapor recovery">Vapor recovery systems</a></li>
</ul>
</li>
<li><b><a href="/wiki/Acid_gas" title="Acid gas">Acid Gas</a>/<a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">SO<sub>2</sub></a> control</b>
<ul>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wet_scrubber" title="Wet scrubber">Wet scrubbers</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Scrubber#Dry_scrubbing" title="Scrubber">Dry scrubbers</a></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Flue-gas_desulfurization" title="Flue-gas desulfurization">Flue-gas desulfurization</a></li>
