<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Regulations">Regulations</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=20" title="Edit section: Regulations">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div class="thumb tright">
<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a href="/wiki/File:Cairo_in_smog.jpg" class="image"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Cairo_in_smog.jpg/220px-Cairo_in_smog.jpg" width="220" height="165" class="thumbimage" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Cairo_in_smog.jpg/330px-Cairo_in_smog.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b7/Cairo_in_smog.jpg/440px-Cairo_in_smog.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1183" data-file-height="887" /></a>
<div class="thumbcaption">
<div class="magnify"><a href="/wiki/File:Cairo_in_smog.jpg" class="internal" title="Enlarge"></a></div>
Smog in <a href="/wiki/Cairo" title="Cairo">Cairo</a></div>
</div>
</div>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Air_quality_law" title="Air quality law">Air quality law</a></div>
<p>In general, there are two types of air quality standards. The first class of standards (such as the U.S. <a href="/wiki/National_Ambient_Air_Quality_Standards" title="National Ambient Air Quality Standards">National Ambient Air Quality Standards</a> and E.U. <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://ec.europa.eu/environment/air/quality/legislation/directive.htm">Air Quality Directive</a>) set maximum atmospheric concentrations for specific pollutants. Environmental agencies enact regulations which are intended to result in attainment of these target levels. The second class (such as the North American <a href="/wiki/Air_Quality_Index" class="mw-redirect" title="Air Quality Index">Air Quality Index</a>) take the form of a scale with various thresholds, which is used to communicate to the public the relative risk of outdoor activity. The scale may or may not distinguish between different pollutants.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Canada">Canada</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=21" title="Edit section: Canada">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>In Canada, air pollution and associated health risks are measured with the <a href="/wiki/Air_Quality_Health_Index_(Canada)" title="Air Quality Health Index (Canada)">Air Quality Health Index</a> or (AQHI). It is a health protection tool used to make decisions to reduce short-term exposure to air pollution by adjusting activity levels during increased levels of air pollution.</p>
<p>The Air Quality Health Index or "AQHI" is a federal program jointly coordinated by <a href="/wiki/Health_Canada" title="Health Canada">Health Canada</a> and <a href="/wiki/Environment_Canada" class="mw-redirect" title="Environment Canada">Environment Canada</a>. However, the AQHI program would not be possible without the commitment and support of the provinces, municipalities and NGOs. From air quality monitoring to health risk communication and community engagement, local partners are responsible for the vast majority of work related to AQHI implementation. The AQHI provides a number from 1 to 10+ to indicate the level of health risk associated with local air quality. Occasionally, when the amount of air pollution is abnormally high, the number may exceed 10. The AQHI provides a local air quality current value as well as a local air quality maximums forecast for today, tonight and tomorrow and provides associated health advice.</p>
<table style="margin:auto; background:Black; color:White;">
<tr style="text-align:center;">
<td style="width:35px; height:35px; background:#9cf;"><b>1</b></td>
<td style="text-align:center; width:35px; height:35px; background:#6cf;"><b>2</b></td>
<td style="text-align:center; width:35px; height:35px; background:#0cf;"><b>3</b></td>
<td style="text-align:center; width:35px; height:35px; background:#9cc;"><b>4</b></td>
<td style="height:35px; height:35px; text-align:center; width:35px; text-align:center; width:35px; background:#999;"><b>5</b></td>
<td style="text-align:center; width:35px; height:35px; background:#996;"><b>6</b></td>
<td style="text-align:center; width:35px; height:35px; background:#960;"><b>7</b></td>
<td style="text-align:center; width:35px; height:35px; background:#963;"><b>8</b></td>
<td style="width:18px; width:35px; text-align:center; text-align:center; height:35px; background:#930;"><b>9</b></td>
<td style="width:18px; width:35px; text-align:center; text-align:center; height:35px; background:#600;"><b>10</b></td>
<td style="text-align:center; width:35px; height:35px; background:#f00;"><b>+</b></td>
</tr>
</table>
<table style="margin:auto; background:white; color:black;">
<tr>
<td><b>Risk:</b></td>
<td>Low <b>(1-3)</b></td>
<td>Moderate <b>(4-6)</b></td>
<td>High <b>(7-10)</b></td>
<td>Very high <b>(above 10)</b></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>As it is now known that even low levels of air pollution can trigger discomfort for the sensitive population, the index has been developed as a continuum: The higher the number, the greater the health risk and need to take precautions. The index describes the level of health risk associated with this number as 'low', 'moderate', 'high' or 'very high', and suggests steps that can be taken to reduce exposure.<sup id="cite_ref-84" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup></p>
<table class="wikitable">
<tr>
<th>Health Risk</th>
<th>Air Quality Health Index</th>
<th colspan="2" style="text-align: center;">Health Messages<sup id="cite_ref-85" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup></th>
</tr>
<tr>
<td></td>
<td></td>
<td><b>At Risk population</b></td>
<td><b>General Population</b></td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><b>Low</b></td>
<td><b>1-3</b></td>
<td><b>Enjoy</b> your usual outdoor activities.</td>
<td><b>Ideal</b> air quality for outdoor activities</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><b>Moderate</b></td>
<td><b>4-6</b></td>
<td><b>Consider reducing</b> or rescheduling strenuous activities outdoors if you are experiencing symptoms.</td>
<td><b>No need to modify</b> your usual outdoor activities unless you experience symptoms such as coughing and throat irritation.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><b>High</b></td>
<td><b>7-10</b></td>
<td><b>Reduce</b> or reschedule strenuous activities outdoors. Children and the elderly should also take it easy.</td>
<td><b>Consider reducing</b> or rescheduling strenuous activities outdoors if you experience symptoms such as coughing and throat irritation.</td>
</tr>
<tr>
<td><b>Very high</b></td>
<td><b>Above 10</b></td>
<td><b>Avoid</b> strenuous activities outdoors. <a href="/wiki/Children" class="mw-redirect" title="Children">Children</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Elderly" class="mw-redirect" title="Elderly">elderly</a> should also avoid outdoor physical exertion and should stay indoors.</td>
<td><b>Reduce</b> or reschedule strenuous activities outdoors, especially if you experience symptoms such as coughing and throat irritation.</td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>The measurement is based on the observed relationship of Nitrogen Dioxide (NO<sub>2</sub>), ground-level Ozone (O<sub>3</sub>) and particulates (PM<sub>2.5</sub>) with mortality, from an analysis of several Canadian cities. Significantly, all three of these pollutants can pose health risks, even at low levels of exposure, especially among those with pre-existing health problems.</p>
<p>When developing the AQHI, Health Canada's original analysis of health effects included five major air pollutants: particulates, <a href="/wiki/Ozone" title="Ozone">ozone</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Nitrogen_dioxide" title="Nitrogen dioxide">nitrogen dioxide</a> (NO2), as well as <a href="/wiki/Sulfur_dioxide" title="Sulfur dioxide">sulfur dioxide</a> (SO<sub>2</sub>), and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_monoxide" title="Carbon monoxide">carbon monoxide</a> (CO). The latter two pollutants provided little information in predicting health effects and were removed from the AQHI formulation.</p>
<p>The AQHI does not measure the effects of odour, <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a>, dust, heat or humidity.</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Germany">Germany</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=22" title="Edit section: Germany">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p><a href="/wiki/TA_Luft" title="TA Luft">TA Luft</a> is the German air quality regulation.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Hotspots">Hotspots</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=23" title="Edit section: Hotspots">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Toxic_Hotspots" class="mw-redirect" title="Toxic Hotspots">Toxic Hotspots</a></div>
<p>Air pollution hotspots are areas where air pollution emissions expose individuals to increased negative health effects.<sup id="cite_ref-86" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup> They are particularly common in highly populated, urban areas, where there may be a combination of stationary sources (e.g. industrial facilities) and mobile sources (e.g. cars and trucks) of pollution. Emissions from these sources can cause respiratory disease, childhood asthma, cancer, and other health problems. Fine particulate matter such as diesel soot, which contributes to more than 3.2 million premature deaths around the world each year, is a significant problem. It is very small and can lodge itself within the lungs and enter the bloodstream. Diesel soot is concentrated in densely populated areas, and one in six people in the U.S. live near a diesel pollution hot spot.<sup id="cite_ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup></p>
<table class="infobox" style="width:22em;width: 220px; float: right; clear: right; margin:0 0 1.0em 1.0em">
<caption>External video</caption>
<tr>
<td colspan="2" style="text-align:center;text-align: left"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/16px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png" width="16" height="16" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/24px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg/32px-Nuvola_apps_kaboodle.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="128" data-file-height="128" /> <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://airvisual.com/earth"><i>AirVisual Earth</i></a> – realtime map of global wind and air pollution <sup id="cite_ref-88" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup></td>
</tr>
</table>
<p>While air pollution hotspots affect a variety of populations, some groups are more likely to be located in hotspots. Previous studies have shown disparities in exposure to pollution by race and/or income. Hazardous land uses (toxic storage and disposal facilities, manufacturing facilities, major roadways) tend to be located where property values and income levels are low. Low socioeconomic status can be a proxy for other kinds of <a href="/wiki/Social_vulnerability" title="Social vulnerability">social vulnerability</a>, including race, a lack of ability to influence regulation and a lack of ability to move to neighborhoods with less environmental pollution. These communities bear a disproportionate burden of environmental pollution and are more likely to face health risks such as cancer or asthma.<sup id="cite_ref-Drury_89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drury-89">[89]</a></sup></p>
<p>Studies show that patterns in race and income disparities not only indicate a higher exposure to pollution but also higher risk of adverse health outcomes.<sup id="cite_ref-Morello-Frosch_2011_90-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morello-Frosch_2011-90">[90]</a></sup> Communities characterized by low socioeconomic status and racial minorities can be more vulnerable to cumulative adverse health impacts resulting from elevated exposure to pollutants than more privileged communities.<sup id="cite_ref-Morello-Frosch_2011_90-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morello-Frosch_2011-90">[90]</a></sup> Blacks and Latinos generally face more pollution than whites and Asians, and low-income communities bear a higher burden of risk than affluent ones.<sup id="cite_ref-Drury_89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Drury-89">[89]</a></sup> Racial discrepancies are particularly distinct in suburban areas of the US South and metropolitan areas of the US West.<sup id="cite_ref-91" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2014)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Residents in public housing, who are generally low-income and cannot move to healthier neighborhoods, are highly affected by nearby refineries and chemical plants.<sup id="cite_ref-92" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup></p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cities">Cities</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=24" title="Edit section: Cities">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<div role="note" class="hatnote">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_most_polluted_cities_in_the_world_by_particulate_matter_concentration" class="mw-redirect" title="List of most polluted cities in the world by particulate matter concentration">List of most polluted cities in the world by particulate matter concentration</a></div>

Deaths from air pollution in 2004</div>

<p>Air pollution is usually concentrated in densely populated metropolitan areas, especially in developing countries where environmental regulations are relatively lax or nonexistent.<sup id="cite_ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> However, even populated areas in developed countries attain unhealthy levels of pollution, with <a href="/wiki/Los_Angeles" title="Los Angeles">Los Angeles</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> being two examples.<sup id="cite_ref-94" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup> Between 2002 and 2011 the incidence of lung cancer in <a href="/wiki/Beijing" title="Beijing">Beijing</a> near doubled. While smoking remains the leading cause of lung cancer in China, the number of smokers is falling while lung cancer rates are rising.<sup id="cite_ref-95" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> Another project focusing on the effects on <a href="/wiki/Air_pollution_on_vegetation" title="Air pollution on vegetation">pollution in vegetation</a> has been researched by the <a href="/wiki/University_of_Sheffield" title="University of Sheffield">local university in Sheffield, UK</a>.</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="National-scale_air_toxics_assessments_1995-2005">National-scale air toxics assessments 1995-2005</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=25" title="Edit section: National-scale air toxics assessments 1995-2005">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>The national-scale air toxics assessment(NATA) is an evaluation of air toxics by the U.S. EPA. EPA has furnished four assessments that characterize nationwide chronic cancer risk estimates and noncancer hazards from inhaling air toxics. The lates was from 2005, and made publicly available in early 2011.</p>
<p>"EPA developed the NATA as a state-of-the-science screening tool for State/Local/Tribal Agencies to prioritize pollutants, emission sources and locations of interest for further study, in order to gain a better understanding of the risks. NATA assessments do not incorporate refined information about emission sources, but rather, use general information about sources to develop estimates of risks which are more likely to overestimate impacts than underestimate them. NATA provides estimates of the risk of cancer and other serious health effects from breathing (inhaling) air toxics in order to inform both national and more localized efforts to identify and prioritize air toxics, emission source types and locations which are of greatest potential concern in terms of contributing to population risk. This in turn helps air pollution experts focus limited analytical resources on areas and or populations where the potential for health risks are highest. Assessments include estimates of cancer and non-cancer health effects based on chronic exposure from outdoor sources, including assessments of non-cancer health effects for Diesel Particulate Matter. Assessments provide a snapshot of the outdoor air quality and the risks to human health that would result if air toxic emissions levels remained unchanged."<sup id="cite_ref-nata_96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-nata-96">[96]</a></sup></p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Governing_urban_air_pollution">Governing urban air pollution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Air_pollution&amp;action=edit&amp;section=26" title="Edit section: Governing urban air pollution">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>In <a href="/wiki/Europe" title="Europe">Europe</a>, Council Directive 96/62/EC on ambient air quality assessment and management provides a common strategy against which <a href="/wiki/Member_states" class="mw-redirect" title="Member states">member states</a> can "set objectives for ambient air quality in order to avoid, prevent or reduce harmful effects on human health and the environment . . . and improve air quality where it is unsatisfactory".<sup id="cite_ref-Europa1996_98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europa1996-98">[98]</a></sup></p>
<p>On 25 July 2008 in the case Dieter Janecek v Freistaat Bayern CURIA, the <a href="/wiki/European_Court_of_Justice" title="European Court of Justice">European Court of Justice</a> ruled that under this directive<sup id="cite_ref-Europa1996_98-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Europa1996-98">[98]</a></sup> citizens have the right to require national authorities to implement a short term action plan that aims to maintain or achieve compliance to air quality limit values.<sup id="cite_ref-CURIA_2008_99-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-CURIA_2008-99">[99]</a></sup></p>
<p>This important <a href="/wiki/Case_law" title="Case law">case law</a> appears to confirm the role of the EC as centralised regulator to European nation-states as regards air pollution control. It places a <a href="/wiki/Supranational" class="mw-redirect" title="Supranational">supranational</a> legal obligation on the UK to protect its citizens from dangerous levels of air pollution, furthermore superseding national interests with those of the citizen.</p>
<p>In 2010, the <a href="/wiki/European_Commission" title="European Commission">European Commission</a> (EC) threatened the UK with legal action against the successive breaching of <a href="/wiki/PM10" class="mw-redirect" title="PM10">PM10</a> limit values.<sup id="cite_ref-100" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> The UK government has identified that if fines are imposed, they could cost the nation upwards of £300 million per year.<sup id="cite_ref-HoC_Air_Quality_Report_2010_101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-HoC_Air_Quality_Report_2010-101">[101]</a></sup></p>
<p>In March 2011, the <a href="/wiki/Greater_London_Built-up_Area" title="Greater London Built-up Area">Greater London Built-up Area</a> remains the only UK region in breach of the EC's limit values, and has been given 3 months to implement an emergency action plan aimed at meeting the EU Air Quality Directive.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian2011_102-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian2011-102">[102]</a></sup> The City of London has dangerous levels of PM10 concentrations, estimated to cause 3000 deaths per year within the city.<sup id="cite_ref-103" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> As well as the threat of EU fines, in 2010 it was threatened with legal action for scrapping the western <a href="/wiki/Congestion_charge" class="mw-redirect" title="Congestion charge">congestion charge</a> zone, which is claimed to have led to an increase in air pollution levels.<sup id="cite_ref-104" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup></p>
<p>In response to these charges, <a href="/wiki/Boris_Johnson" title="Boris Johnson">Boris Johnson</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mayor_of_London" title="Mayor of London">Mayor of London</a>, has criticised the current need for European cities to communicate with Europe through their nation state's <a href="/wiki/Central_government" title="Central government">central government</a>, arguing that in future "A great city like London" should be permitted to bypass its government and deal directly with the European Commission regarding its air quality action plan.<sup id="cite_ref-Guardian2011_102-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Guardian2011-102">[102]</a></sup></p>
<p>This can be interpreted as recognition that cities can transcend the traditional national government organisational hierarchy and develop solutions to air pollution using global governance networks, for example through transnational relations. Transnational relations include but are not exclusive to national governments and intergovernmental organisations,<sup id="cite_ref-Kappen_105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kappen-105">[105]</a></sup> allowing sub-national actors including cities and <a href="/wiki/Regions" class="mw-redirect" title="Regions">regions</a> to partake in air pollution control as independent actors.</p>
<p>Particularly promising at present are global city partnerships.<sup id="cite_ref-pattberg_106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pattberg-106">[106]</a></sup> These can be built into networks, for example the <a href="/wiki/C40_Cities_Climate_Leadership_Group" title="C40 Cities Climate Leadership Group">C40 Cities Climate Leadership Group</a>, of which London is a member. The C40 is a public 'non-state' network of the world's leading cities that aims to curb their greenhouse emissions.<sup id="cite_ref-pattberg_106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pattberg-106">[106]</a></sup> The C40 has been identified as 'governance from the middle' and is an alternative to intergovernmental policy.<sup id="cite_ref-107" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup> It has the potential to improve urban air quality as participating cities "exchange information, learn from best practices and consequently mitigate carbon dioxide emissions independently from national government decisions".<sup id="cite_ref-pattberg_106-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pattberg-106">[106]</a></sup> A criticism of the C40 network is that its exclusive nature limits influence to participating cities and risks drawing resources away from less powerful city and regional actors.</p>
